import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class ShuffleNetUnit(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, groups=3):
        """
        ShuffleNet unit implementation.

        :param in_channels: Number of input channels.
        :param out_channels: Number of output channels.
        :param groups: Number of groups for group convolution.
        """
        super(ShuffleNetUnit, self).__init__()
        
        # Ensure the output channels are divisible by groups
        assert out_channels % 4 == 0
        mid_channels = out_channels // 4
        
        # First 1x1 group convolution
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, mid_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, groups=groups, bias=False)
        self.bn1 = nn.BatchNorm2d(mid_channels)
        
        # Depthwise 3x3 convolution
        self.conv2 = nn.Conv2d(mid_channels, mid_channels, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1, groups=mid_channels, bias=False)
        self.bn2 = nn.BatchNorm2d(mid_channels)
        
        # Second 1x1 group convolution
        self.conv3 = nn.Conv2d(mid_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, groups=groups, bias=False)
        self.bn3 = nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels)
        
        # Shuffle operation
        self.shuffle = ChannelShuffle(groups)
        
        # Shortcut connection if input and output channels are the same
        if in_channels == out_channels:
            self.shortcut = nn.Sequential()
        else:
            self.shortcut = nn.Sequential(
                nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False),
                nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels)
            )
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass for ShuffleNet unit.

        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, out_channels, height, width)
        """
        out = F.relu(self.bn1(self.conv1(x)))
        out = self.bn2(self.conv2(out))
        out = self.shuffle(out)
        out = F.relu(self.bn3(self.conv3(out)))
        
        out += self.shortcut(x)
        return out

class ChannelShuffle(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, groups):
        """
        Channel shuffle operation.

        :param groups: Number of groups for shuffling.
        """
        super(ChannelShuffle, self).__init__()
        self.groups = groups
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass for channel shuffle.

        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, channels, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, channels, height, width)
        """
        batch_size, channels, height, width = x.size()
        channels_per_group = channels // self.groups
        
        # Reshape
        x = x.view(batch_size, self.groups, channels_per_group, height, width)
        
        # Transpose
        x = x.transpose(1, 2).contiguous()
        
        # Flatten
        x = x.view(batch_size, -1, height, width)
        
        return x

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000, groups=3, stages_repeats=[3, 7, 3], stages_out_channels=[24, 240, 480, 960]):
        """
        ShuffleNet architecture.

        :param num_classes: Number of output classes.
        :param groups: Number of groups for group convolution.
        :param stages_repeats: List of ints specifying the number of repeats for each stage.
        :param stages_out_channels: List of ints specifying the output channels for each stage.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(3, stages_out_channels[0], kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1, bias=False)
        self.bn1 = nn.BatchNorm2d(stages_out_channels[0])
        self.maxpool = nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1)
        
        self.stage2 = self._make_stage(stages_out_channels[0], stages_out_channels[1], stages_repeats[0], groups)
        self.stage3 = self._make_stage(stages_out_channels[1], stages_out_channels[2], stages_repeats[1], groups)
        self.stage4 = self._make_stage(stages_out_channels[2], stages_out_channels[3], stages_repeats[2], groups)
        
        self.conv5 = nn.Conv2d(stages_out_channels[3], 1024, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False)
        self.bn5 = nn.BatchNorm2d(1024)
        
        self.fc = nn.Linear(1024, num_classes)
    
    def _make_stage(self, in_channels, out_channels, repeats, groups):
        """
        Helper function to create a stage of ShuffleNet units.

        :param in_channels: Number of input channels.
        :param out_channels: Number of output channels.
        :param repeats: Number of ShuffleNet units in the stage.
        :param groups: Number of groups for group convolution.
        :return: nn.Sequential containing the stage.
        """
        layers = []
        layers.append(ShuffleNetUnit(in_channels, out_channels, groups))
        for _ in range(1, repeats):
            layers.append(ShuffleNetUnit(out_channels, out_channels, groups))
        return nn.Sequential(*layers)
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass for ShuffleNet.

        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, 3, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        x = F.relu(self.bn1(self.conv1(x)))
        x = self.maxpool(x)
        
        x = self.stage2(x)
        x = self.stage3(x)
        x = self.stage4(x)
        
        x = F.relu(self.bn5(self.conv5(x)))
        x = F.adaptive_avg_pool2d(x, (1, 1))
        x = x.view(x.size(0), -1)
        x = self.fc(x)
        
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 10
input_channels = 3
height = 224
width = 224
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, input_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

Total blocks 31360 is fine, but if each block has 960 threads, each SM can run at most 2 blocks (2048 threads limit), so 1920 threads. That's okay. But register pressure might limit to 1 block per SM. Alternatively, we can process one spatial location per thread (not block), and use a simpler elementwise kernel for each layer. But that goes back to separate kernels. Wait, there is another possibility: cuDNN group conv and depthwise conv are already very fast. The reference might be spending a lot of time on memory transfers between ops. But PyTorch's JIT can fuse some ops? The reference is eager mode. So fusing BN+ReLU into conv weights and trying to keep operations in-place might reduce memory traffic but the PyTorch autograd overhead is not present because we only do forward. A more pragmatic approach: 1. Convert all Conv+BN into a single Conv with fused weights and bias. 2. For each unit, compute: `x1 = F.relu(F.conv2d(x, w1, b1, groups=g))` `x2 = F.conv2d(x1, w2, b2, groups=mid_c)` `x2 = channel_shuffle(x2)` `x3 = F.relu(F.conv2d(x2, w3, b3, groups=g))` `out = x3 + (F.conv2d(x, ws, bs) if ... else x)` Here, `channel_shuffle` is just a view + transpose. In PyTorch, if we do `.view(...).transpose(...).reshape(...)`, it might create a non-contiguous tensor. Does `F.conv2d` handle non-contiguous inputs? Yes, it does. But the performance might degrade slightly due to strided memory access. However, we can write a custom shuffle kernel that writes contiguous output, but that's one kernel launch. Or we can reorder weights of conv3 to account for the shuffle, making the shuffle a no-op! Let's think about that. Channel shuffle permutes the channel dimension. The subsequent conv3 is group conv with `groups=3`. If we permute the weights of conv3 accordingly, we can skip the shuffle! Suppose conv2 has `mid_c` output channels, which are then shuffled. The channels are conceptually grouped into `g` groups of `mid_c/g`. After shuffle, the channels are interleaved: e.g., for g=3, cpg=20. Original order: [0..19, 20..39, 40..59]. After shuffle: [0,20,40, 1,21,41, ...]. The conv3 groups take contiguous chunks of `mid_c/g` input channels. So group 0 gets channels [0,20,40,...] which were originally channel 0 from each group of conv2. Group 1 gets [1,21,41,...], etc. If we instead keep the tensor in the original order (no shuffle), and rearrange the weights of conv3 such that group 0 operates on channels [0,20,40,...] effectively. But weight layout for group conv is `[out_c, in_c/g, kH, kW]`. For group 0, the weight matrix is `[out_c/g, mid_c/g, 1, 1]`. It expects input channels `0..mid_c/g - 1` (i.e., 0..19) to be contiguous in memory. If we skip the shuffle, the input channels for group 0 are physically `0..19` (original conv2 group 0). But mathematically, after shuffle, group 0 should see channels [0,20,40,...]. To emulate this without shuffle, we need conv3 to be a fully connected mix? No, group conv restricts connections. Shuffle is what enables cross-group information flow. If we skip the shuffle, we need to change the weight layout, but the group conv groups are fixed to contiguous channel blocks. We cannot map non-contiguous channels to a group. Unless we physically reorder the channels in the weight tensor and keep the input contiguous. Wait: The shuffle is a permutation of the channels in the activation tensor. Let P be the permutation matrix. The operation is `y = conv3(P * x)`, where `conv3` is a group conv. Can we write `y = conv3'(x)` where `conv3'` has permuted weights? For 1x1 conv, `y = W * (P * x) = (W * P) * x`. Since `W` is block diagonal (due to groups), `W * P` is not block diagonal in general. But we can still absorb `P` into `W` by permuting the columns of `W`. However, after absorbing `P`, the resulting weight matrix `W' = W * P` will have columns permuted. But `W'` is no longer block-diagonal with the same group structure. This means we cannot use group conv anymore; we need a dense conv or a different grouping. But the model defines `conv3` as group conv with `groups=3`. Can we fuse the shuffle into conv weights and still perform it as a group conv? No, because the permutation P interleaves columns from different blocks of W. So W' = W * P would require that the input channels are contiguous. But W' is not block-diagonal: each output group has connections to input channels from all groups (because P reorders them). Wait, let's check: - W is block diagonal: block g connects output group g to input group g. - P shuffles channels: it groups channel 0 from each input group together, then channel 1, etc. - W * P: For output group 0, originally it only connected to input group 0. After applying P, the inputs are permuted. But W still only has non-zero weights on columns corresponding to input group 0. The permutation P selects specific columns from the original input. But W*P will have non-zero entries only in columns that were originally in input group 0. Since P reorders the input, those columns now appear scattered. But W*P is just a permuted version of W. However, when we apply W'*x, we are doing a dense matrix multiply. Is it equivalent to group_conv(W, P*x)? Yes, mathematically. But can we implement it efficiently as a group conv? The input x is still in the original (unshuffled) order. The weights W' have the same non-zero pattern as W but with columns permuted. A standard group conv implementation expects the weights to be block-diagonal and contiguous per block. If we store W' as a block-diagonal weight matrix but with a non-contiguous access pattern to x, that defeats the purpose. In short, we cannot fuse the shuffle into conv3 weights without either (a) changing the operation type from group conv to dense conv, or (b) permuting the input x. So the shuffle must happen as a tensor reordering. But maybe we can write a custom kernel that does the shuffle plus the next conv3 in one go, reading from the unshuffled memory in the shuffled order. That is, the conv3 kernel reads `mid_c` channels in permuted order as its input. Similarly, conv2 is depthwise on `mid_c`. Its output is `mid_c`. Then we shuffle that. So we can write a kernel that does: compute depthwise conv2 output (mid_c), and then write it to shared memory / registers in shuffled order, then compute conv3. So we can fuse conv2 + shuffle + conv3. But conv1 also produces `mid_c`, then relu, then conv2 reads it. If we fuse everything in one unit, we don't need to write conv1 output to global memory. That's huge. However, the unit is only part of a Sequential stage. The output of one unit is the input to the next. So we cannot fuse across units without writing to global memory, unless we fuse the entire stage. But the stage has 3-7 units. Fusing 7 units in one kernel might be possible but requires maximum recursion of operations and large register usage. But perhaps fusing just one unit is enough to get speedup, and the rest of the overhead remains. We can implement a generic ShuffleNetUnit fused kernel and call it for each unit. This would replace the many small ops in a unit with just 4 global memory accesses: read input, read weights (cached), write output, and read shortcut input (same as input). Plus the 3x3 depthwise reads neighbors from global memory (or shared memory if we tile). This is much better than the reference which does: - conv1 (GEMM, write mid) - bn1 (read mid, write mid) - relu (read mid, write mid) - conv2 (GEMM, write mid2) - bn2 (read mid2, write mid2) - shuffle (read mid2, write mid2_shuffled) [contiguous()] - conv3 (GEMM, write out) - bn3 (read out, write out) - relu (read out, write out) - shortcut conv (read x, write s) - shortcut bn (read s, write s) - add (read out, read s, write final) Total: ~12 global memory read/write passes of the feature maps. The fused kernel does ~2 passes (read x, write final) plus some neighbor reads for depthwise. That's a 6x memory bandwidth reduction. Since these layers are likely memory bandwidth bound (especially the 1x1 pointwise convs with low arithmetic intensity), this could give significant speedup. So our plan: 1. In `ModelNew.__init__`, convert all `Conv+BN` and `Conv+BN+ReLU` into pre-fused weights/biases. 2. The kernel should handle: - Loading input pixel x[b, c_in, h, w] and its channels. - Computing 1x1 group conv + ReLU. - Computing 3x3 depthwise conv. - Channel shuffle (reordering of `mid_c` elements). - Computing 1x1 group conv + ReLU. - Shortcut: if in_c != out_c, 1x1 conv + bias. else identity. - Adding shortcut. - Writing output. 4. This is ambitious but possible. **Tile-based design:** We partition the H*W spatial domain into tiles, e.g., `TILE_H = 8`, `TILE_W = 8`. Each block processes one tile of `TILE_H x TILE_W` pixels for one image (one b). However, to compute depthwise 3x3, the tile needs a halo of 1 pixel on each side. So the block loads `(TILE_H+2) x (TILE_W+2) x in_c` values for `conv1`, computes `mid` for the whole tile + halo, stores `mid` in shared memory, then computes depthwise for the inner tile, storing `mid2` in shared memory/registers, then shuffles, then computes `conv3`, then shortcut and add. If we reduce the number of launches by fusing, we can save that overhead. **A practical plan that balances complexity and speedup:** 1. **Fuse Conv+BN+ReLU weights:** Preprocess all weights so that every `Conv->BN->ReLU` becomes a single convolution weight + bias. Every `Conv->BN` (without ReLU) becomes weight + bias. This eliminates the separate BN and ReLU kernels. 2. **Channel Shuffle as custom kernel:** Instead of `.view().transpose().contiguous().view()`, write a simple channel shuffle kernel that reads and writes contiguously. No. Actually, a much simpler approach: replace the `forward` of `ShuffleNetUnit` with a single custom kernel that calls `cudnn` internally? Not possible from CUDA. Or use `torch.matmul` for 1x1 convs. Wait! A critical insight: 1x1 group convolution is equivalent to `einsum` or `bmm` on reshaped tensors. Specifically, for input `x` of shape `[B, in_c, H, W]`, weights `w` of shape `[out_c, in_c/g, 1, 1]`. Reshape `x` -> `[B, g, in_c/g, H, W]`. Reshape `w` -> `[g, out_c/g, in_c/g]`. Then group conv is `einsum('b g i h w, g o i -> b g o h w')`. Then reshape to `[B, out_c, H, W]`. This can be done with `torch.matmul`: `x_reshaped = x.view(B, g, in_c//g, H*W)` `w_reshaped = w.view(g, out_c//g, in_c//g)` `out_reshaped = torch.einsum('g o i, b g i n -> b g o n', w_reshaped, x_reshaped)` or `torch.matmul(w_reshaped, x_reshaped)` if dimensions match. `x_reshaped` shape: `[B, g, in_c/g, N]` where N=H*W. `w_reshaped` shape: `[g, out_c/g, in_c/g]`. We want: for each b,g,n: `out[b, g, :, n] = w[g] @ x[b, g, :, n]`. This is a batch matrix multiply: `w_reshaped` [g, out/g, in/g], `x_reshaped` [B, g, in/g, N] -> we can permute x to `[g, B, in/g, N]` and do `bmm(w_reshaped.unsqueeze(1), x_permuted)` -> `[g, B, out/g, N]`. Then permute back. This uses cuBLAS GEMM (very fast). For 1x1 conv, this might be comparable or better than cuDNN group conv. And it allows us to easily fuse with bias and relu by writing a custom kernel or using `torch.addmv` / `torch.relu_` on the result. Similarly, depthwise 3x3 conv is `groups=mid_c`. This can be viewed as: `x_reshaped = x.view(B*mid_c, 1, H, W)` `w_reshaped = w.view(mid_c, 1, 3, 3)` `out = F.conv2d(x_reshaped, w_reshaped, groups=B*mid_c, padding=1)`? Wait, groups can't be B*mid_c with input channels=1 and output channels=1 per group. Actually standard depthwise is `groups=mid_c` on input `[B, mid_c, H, W]`. There is no easy GEMM formulation without im2col. But we can write a simple depthwise kernel since it's relatively cheap. However, the `einsum` approach for 1x1 conv gives us flexibility to fuse with subsequent ops. Let's assess the feasibility of writing a kernel that: - Takes input x (global memory). - Writes output y (global memory). - Uses a temporary global memory buffer for `mid` (since depthwise needs neighbors). - Has parameters for all weights/biases. - Internally, each block processes one pixel. - Step 1: compute `mid` for the pixel (conv1+bias+relu) and write to global `mid` buffer. - Step 2: wait for all blocks to finish writing `mid`. Then compute `conv2` for the pixel by reading `mid` neighbors. - Step 3: compute `conv3`, `shortcut`, `add`, `relu`, write to output. We need a global synchronization between step 1 and 2 within the same kernel. That's not possible across blocks except by ending the kernel. So we need **two kernels per unit**. Kernel A (conv1): per pixel, reads x, computes conv1+bias+relu, writes mid. Kernel B (rest): per pixel, reads mid (neighbors), x, computes conv2+shuffle+conv3+bias+relu + shortcut(x) + add, writes final. This reduces each unit from ~12 kernel launches to 2. Plus we have pre-fused weights so there are no separate BN/ReLU kernels. The shuffle is just channel reordering in registers. Total kernel launches: initial conv1+bn+relu (could be 1, fused), maxpool (1), + 2 per unit (26 for 13 units) + final conv5+bn+relu (1), avgpool (1), fc (1). Total ~32 kernel launches vs ~150+. That's a big reduction. And memory traffic is also reduced: only x, mid, out are read/written. Can we further reduce by fusing maxpool with conv1? conv1 has stride 2 (112x112). Let's think about memory layout. `mid` is `[B, mid_c, H, W]`. So for a fixed (b,h,w), the address of `mid[b, c, h, w]` is `(b*mid_c + c)*H*W + h*W + w`. For neighbor `(b, c, h+kh, w+kw)`, address is `(b*mid_c + c)*H*W + (h+kh)*W + (w+kw)`. For a given `c`, the difference between neighbor and center is `(kh*W + kw)`. For a given thread handling `c`, reading 9 neighbors means 9 loads with strides of `W` or `1` within the same channel. That's fine. So memory access is **not coalesced** across threads for the same spatial neighbor. This is bad. To get coalescing, adjacent threads should process adjacent spatial pixels, not adjacent channels. For (c=0,g=0): addr 0 -> c0. (c=0,g=1): addr 2 -> c2. (c=0,g=2): addr 4 -> c4. (c=1,g=0): addr 1 -> c1. (c=1,g=1): addr 3 -> c3. (c=1,g=2): addr 5 -> c5. So logical order of `t2` is [c0, c2, c4, c1, c3, c5]. This is the shuffled order. Now view(1,6,1,1) on `t2`. The merge dims 1(cpg=2) and 2(g=3) into size 6. Stride of dim1 = 1. Size = 2. Stride of dim2 = 2. `1*2 == 2`. Yes. Merged stride = 1. Result `r` strides: (6, 1, 1, 1). `r[0, k, 0, 0]` address = k. For k=0: addr 0 -> c0. k=1: addr 1 -> c1. k=2: addr 2 -> c2. ... So `r` is contiguous in memory and reads [c0, c1, c2, c3, c4, c5]. But logically, the shuffle view says `r[0,0]` should be `t2[0,0,0]` = c0. `r[0,1]` should be `t2[0,1,0]` = c2? Wait, no. `view` doesn't change the logical mapping based on strides? It does. After view, `r` is a new tensor sharing storage with `t2`. `r[0, k, h, w]` = `t2[0, k%2, k//2, h, w]`. So for k=0: `t2[0,0,0]` = c0. addr=0. k=1: `t2[0,1,0]` = c1. addr=1. k=2: `t2[0,0,1]` = c2. addr=2. k=3: `t2[0,1,1]` = c3. addr=3. k=4: `t2[0,0,2]` = c4. addr=4. k=5: `t2[0,1,2]` = c5. addr=5. This means `r` reads memory in the order [c0, c1, c2, c3, c4, c5], which is the original order. The *logical* indices of `r` correspond to the original channels. So the "shuffle" is completely lost! The `.view()` after `.transpose()` on a contiguous tensor cancels the transpose because the dimensions are compatible for merge. Wait, this cannot be right. The reference must actually do a shuffle. Let's test in Python. ```python import torch B, C, H, W = 1, 6, 1, 1 groups = 3 x = torch.arange(B*C*H*W).reshape(B, C, H, W) cpg = C // groups y = x.view(B, groups, cpg, H, W).transpose(1,2).contiguous().view(B, -1, H, W) print(x) print(y) ``` Let's mentally run: x = [[[ [0] ], [ [1] ], [ [2] ], [ [3] ], [ [4] ], [ [5] ]]] view(1,3,2,1,1): group0: channels 0,1 -> 0,1 group1: channels 2,3 -> 2,3 group2: channels 4,5 -> 4,5 transpose(1,2): shape (1,2,3,1,1) dim1=0: group0[0], group1[0], group2[0] -> 0, 2, 4 dim1=1: group0[1], group1[1], group2[1] -> 1, 3, 5 After `.contiguous()`, memory is [0,2,4,1,3,5]. view(1,6,1,1): logical order is [0, 2, 4, 1, 3, 5]. This IS the shuffled order. The `.contiguous()` is necessary to materialize the transposed layout into a new contiguous buffer with that order. If we skip `.contiguous()`, we have a non-contiguous view with strides. The logical order is still [0,2,4,1,3,5], but the memory is [0,1,2,3,4,5]. Does `F.conv2d` on this non-contiguous tensor compute the convolution correctly with the *logical* ordering? Yes! Convolution operates on the tensor values at their logical positions, regardless of underlying physical layout. The underlying memory layout only affects performance. So we can **skip `.contiguous()`**! The resulting tensor is non-contiguous (strides `C*H*W, H*W, W, 1`? Wait no, if we skip `.contiguous()`, the strides after `view` on the transposed tensor are `(C*H*W, H*W, cpg*H*W, W, 1)`? Let's recompute. After transpose, strides: `(C*H*W, H*W, cpg*H*W, W, 1)`. Wait for the small example: C=6, H=W=1. Strides = (6, 1, 2, 1, 1). In the general formula, `cpg*H*W` vs `H*W`. For H=W=1, `cpg*H*W = 2`, `H*W = 1`. So strides are `(6, 1, 2, 1, 1)`. Then `view(B, C, H, W)` merges dims 1 and 2 into size C. The merged stride is `stride[1] = H*W` (if we follow view rules). So result strides: `(C*H*W, H*W, W, 1)`. For small example: `(6, 1, 1, 1)`. Wait, `H*W = 1`. So the resulting tensor has strides `(6, 1, 1, 1)`, which is contiguous! But the underlying data is still [0,1,2,3,4,5]. And `r[0,1,0,0]` address = base + 1, which is `1` (original channel 1). But logically, after shuffle without contiguous, `r[0,1,0,0]` should correspond to the second channel in the shuffled order, which is `2` (original channel 2). This is the contradiction: view on transposed contiguous tensor when dimensions satisfy the merge condition results in a tensor that is indistinguishable from the original contiguous tensor. So the shuffle is lost. Let's check PyTorch's documentation or behavior. Actually, there is a known issue: reshaping a transposed tensor can only be done if the dimensions are "compatible". In this case, they are compatible, but the resulting tensor is a view that does NOT perform the transpose. It just collapses dimensions. Wait, that's not true. Let's manually compute a more concrete case. ```python x = torch.arange(6).reshape(1,3,2,1,1) # x[0,0,0,0,0] = 0, x[0,0,1,0,0] = 1, x[0,1,0,0,0] = 2, x[0,1,1,0,0] = 3, x[0,2,0,0,0] = 4, x[0,2,1,0,0] = 5 y = x.transpose(1,2) # shape (1,2,3,1,1) # y[0,0,0,0,0] = x[0,0,0,0,0] = 0 # y[0,0,1,0,0] = x[0,1,0,0,0] = 2 # y[0,0,2,0,0] = x[0,2,0,0,0] = 4 # y[0,1,0,0,0] = x[0,0,1,0,0] = 1 # y[0,1,1,0,0] = x[0,1,1,0,0] = 3 # y[0,1,2,0,0] = x[0,2,1,0,0] = 5 z = y.view(1,6,1,1) # z[0,0] = y[0,0,0] = 0 # z[0,1] = y[0,1,0] = 1 # z[0,2] = y[0,0,1] = 2 # z[0,3] = y[0,1,1] = 3 # z[0,4] = y[0,0,2] = 4 # z[0,5] = y[0,1,2] = 5 ``` So z = [0,1,2,3,4,5]! The transpose is lost because the view merges dimensions in a way that "undoes" the transpose. This is a known pitfall. The `.contiguous()` is **essential** to preserve the shuffled order. Because without it, `view` just sees that the transposed dimensions are compatible for merge and collapses them back to the original order. So we **must** do a permutation that actually shuffles the data. In the reference, `.contiguous()` does a copy. We can replace it with a fast custom kernel that does the channel shuffle. That avoids the overhead of PyTorch's general-purpose `contiguous()` (which might be generic but not necessarily slow, but still a separate kernel launch). So we need a `channel_shuffle` kernel. Now, what is the fastest way to implement the entire unit? Idea: write a custom `shufflenet_unit` kernel that takes input `x`, computes everything, and writes output `y`. Since conv1 and conv3 are 1x1 group and conv2 is 3x3 depthwise, and we have to write `mid` to global memory (for depthwise spatial neighbors), we use **two** custom kernels per unit: `unit_conv1` and `unit_rest`. `unit_conv1`: per pixel, 1x1 group conv + bias + ReLU -> write `mid`. `unit_rest`: per pixel, read `mid` (neighbors), depthwise 3x3 + bias, channel shuffle in registers, 1x1 group conv + bias + ReLU, plus optional shortcut 1x1 conv + bias, add -> write output. We can implement these using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. **Kernel design details:** Let's fix the block configuration. To get coalescing, we want each thread to handle one spatial pixel, and adjacent threads in a warp to handle adjacent pixels in a row. Block size = `TILE_W` (e.g., 256). So 1D block of 256 threads. Each block handles 256 contiguous pixels in row-major order. Grid = ceil(B*H*W / 256). For a thread, compute `pixel_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x`. Map to `b, h, w`. `b = pixel_idx / (H*W)`, `rem = pixel_idx % (H*W)`, `h = rem / W`, `w = rem % W`. **Kernel A: `shufflenet_conv1_relu`** Parameters: - `float* out` (mid) - `const float* in` (x) - `const float* weight`, `const float* bias` - `int B, H, W, in_c, out_c, groups` Compute `pixel_idx`. If out of range, return. Compute `base_in = ((b * in_c) * H + h) * W + w`. Compute `base_out = ((b * out_c) * H + h) * W + w`. `in_c_per_g = in_c / groups;` `out_c_per_g = out_c / groups;` Each thread loops over `o = 0 .. out_c-1`: `g = o / out_c_per_g;` `sum = bias[o];` `in_ptr = base_in + g * in_c_per_g * H * W;` `w_ptr = o * in_c_per_g;` For `i = 0 .. in_c_per_g - 1`: `sum += in[in_ptr + i * H * W] * weight[w_ptr + i];` `out[base_out + o * H * W] = max(sum, 0.0f);` Wait, is this coalesced? Adjacent threads (in warp) have same `o`? No, they all loop over `o`. But if we have threads process different `o`, memory access for `in` is strided by `H*W` across threads. Better: each thread handles **all** output channels for its pixel. Two loops: - Outer loop over output channel groups or channels. - But the memory access to `in` for a given `i` across threads: thread 0 reads `in[base_in0 + i*H*W]`, thread 1 reads `in[base_in1 + i*H*W]`. Since `base_in0` and `base_in1` are adjacent pixel addresses (differ by 1), the loads are coalesced! Because for a fixed `i`, all threads read the same channel `i` but different spatial locations, which are adjacent in memory (NCHW: channel `i` pixels are contiguous). So the loads within the `i` loop are coalesced across the warp. Good. Similarly, writes to `out` for a fixed `o` are coalesced across threads. So the simple per-thread-all-channels loop is actually coalesced for spatial access. Perfect. Performance of Kernel A: - For each pixel, `out_c * in_c/groups` FMAs. - For 240->240 unit: `240 * 80 = 19,200` FMAs per pixel. Wait, conv1 is in_c -> mid_c. For 240->240, in_c=240, mid_c=60. `60 * 80 = 4,800` FMAs. - At 2 TFLOPS per SM? Actually fp32 peak is 19.5 TFLOPS on A100. Per pixel 4.8K FMAs = 9.6K FLOPs. 31,360 pixels = 301M FLOPs. At peak that's 15 microseconds. Memory bandwidth: read x (240ch), write mid (60ch), read w1 (60*80=4.8K). x and mid dominate: (240+60)*4 bytes * 31k = 37.5MB. At 2TB/s = 19us. So compute and memory are comparable. This kernel is likely memory-bound or near balance. **Kernel B: `shufflenet_unit_rest`** Parameters: - `float* out` - `const float* mid` (input to conv2) - `const float* x_orig` (for shortcut) - `const float* w2, b2` - `const float* w3, b3` - `const float* ws, bs` (shortcut, if used) - `int B, H, W, mid_c, out_c, groups` - `bool has_shortcut` For each pixel (b,h,w): 1. Compute `mid_c` depthwise values `mid2[c]`. For each `c` in `0..mid_c-1`: `val = b2[c];` For `kh=-1..1`, `kw=-1..1`: `h2 = h+kh; w2 = w+kw;` if in bounds: `idx = ((b*mid_c + c)*H + h2)*W + w2;` `val += mid[idx] * w2[c*9 + (kh+1)*3 + (kw+1)];` `mid2[c] = val;` Note: `mid` loads are coalesced across threads for fixed `c` because adjacent threads read adjacent pixels (`h2*W+w2` differs by 1). Threads loop over `c` sequentially. For each `c`, all threads do a coalesced load of the neighbor. Then c increments, and they load the next channel's neighbor, which is `H*W` away. This is not coalesced across `c` iterations, but since all threads in the warp are at the same `c` at the same time, they load the same channel for different pixels, which is coalesced within the warp. Good. 2. Channel shuffle `mid2` channels in registers. Need a temporary array `mid2_shuffled[mid_c]`. Since `mid_c <= 240`, we can store it in a local array. Will the compiler put it in registers? 240 floats is a lot (240*4=960 bytes per thread). Max registers per thread on Ampere is 255 (32-bit) = 1020 bytes. 240 floats uses 240 registers. Plus other variables. This might spill to local memory. Local memory is cached L1/L2, so it's not terrible but slower than registers. To avoid register pressure, we can compute conv3 directly from the shuffled indices without explicitly reordering. For conv3 (1x1 group, mid_c -> out_c), output group `g` reads input channels `g * (mid_c/groups) + i` IF the tensor were contiguous. But after shuffle, the channel order is changed. Let's derive the mapping: Original `mid2` channels: `0..mid_c-1`. Shuffled order: for `groups=g`, `cpg = mid_c / g`. Shuffled channel `c'` corresponds to original channel ` (c' % g) * cpg + (c' // g) `. Let's verify: g=3, cpg=20. c'=0 -> orig 0*20 + 0 = 0. Correct. c'=1 -> orig 1*20 + 0 = 20. Correct. c'=2 -> orig 2*20 + 0 = 40. Correct. c'=3 -> orig 0*20 + 1 = 1. Correct. So `orig_c = (c' % g) * cpg + (c' / g)`. Equivalently, if we have the original `mid2` array, to get the shuffled value at logical index `c'`, we read `mid2[ (c' % g) * cpg + (c' / g) ]`. So in the conv3 loop, instead of shuffling the array, we can use this indexing. Conv3: for each output channel `o`, group `go = o / out_c_per_g`. It reads `mid_c/groups` input channels. The input channel index within the group is `i`. The logical input channel to conv3 is `go * mid_c_per_g + i`. The corresponding shuffled original channel is: `c_shuffled = ( (go * mid_c_per_g + i) % groups ) * cpg + ( (go * mid_c_per_g + i) / groups )`. Since `go * mid_c_per_g` is a multiple of `mid_c_per_g`. And `mid_c = groups * cpg`. `mid_c_per_g = cpg`. So `go * cpg + i`. `% groups` depends on `go*cpg + i`. This is not trivially simplified because `cpg` may not be a multiple of `groups`. For example, g=3, cpg=20. `go=0`: indices `0..19`. `%3`: 0,1,2,0,1,2... `/3`: 0,0,0,1,1,1... So `orig_c = (index%3)*20 + index/3`. This indeed interleaves. So in the conv3 dot product, for a given `o` in group `go`, and input index `i` within group: `idx_in_group = go * cpg + i;` `orig_c = (idx_in_group % groups) * cpg + (idx_in_group / groups);` `val += mid2[orig_c] * w3[o * cpg + i];` This avoids an explicit shuffle array, at the cost of a modulo and division per multiply. Since `mid_c` is small (<=240) and `groups` is 3, division by 3 is fast. Or we can precompute a lookup table `shuffle_idx[cpg]` per group in shared memory or constant memory. But `cpg` varies (20, 40, 60, 80, ... up to 240/3=80). We can compute the mapping on the fly. Actually, we can precompute `shuffle_idx` in shared memory for the block. `mid_c` max 240. We can have an integer array of size 240 in shared memory. Each thread loads nothing; we can have thread 0 compute it? Or just compute it in registers per thread. The cost of `%` and `/` by a small constant is optimized by compiler to multiplies. So it's okay. 3. Compute `out[o] = relu(b3[o] + dot(w3[o], shuffled_mid))` for each `o`. Loop `o = 0..out_c-1`. Each thread handles one pixel and loops over all `o`. Inside loop, compute dot product over `mid_c_per_g` inputs. 4. Compute shortcut: If `has_shortcut`: `ws` is 1x1 conv `in_c -> out_c`. `bs` is bias. Dot product over `in_c` inputs from `x_orig`. Else: use `x_orig` directly (identity). 5. `out[o] += shortcut[o]`. 6. Write `out` to global memory. Kernel B FLOPs per pixel for 240->240 unit: - Depthwise: `240 * 9 = 2,160` FMAs. - Conv3: `240 * (240/3=80) = 19,200` FMAs. - Shortcut identity: 0 FMAs. Total ~21K FMAs. Memory: read mid (240ch * 9 neighbors avg ~8? actually exactly 9 loads, but boundary ones skip some; ignore boundary). Effective read ~ mid_c * 9 * 4 bytes. `240*9*4 = 8640 bytes`. But we also read `w2`. The dominant memory is reading `mid` 9 times per channel. Wait, `mid` is `[B, mid_c, H, W]`. For each pixel and each channel, 9 loads. Total `mid` bytes read per pixel = `mid_c * 9 * 4 = 8640`. But there are boundaries where less than 9. Average ~8. For 31,360 pixels: `31,360 * 8640 ≈ 271MB`. Plus writing out `960ch` = 120MB. Plus reading `x` for shortcut (if identity, need x 960ch = 120MB). Total ~511MB. At 2TB/s = 256 microseconds. Plus compute. Reference would have done this as multiple passes totaling >1GB traffic. So kernel B is ~2x faster on memory alone. For the first unit in a stage (e.g., 24->240): - Depthwise: 60*9 = 540 FMAs. - Conv3: 240 * (60/3=20) = 4,800 FMAs. - Shortcut: 1x1 conv 24->240 = 5,760 FMAs. Total ~11K FMAs. Memory: read mid (60ch * 9 = 540 floats = 2.1KB). Read x for shortcut (24ch = 96 bytes? per pixel). Write out (240ch = 960 bytes). Total ~3KB per pixel. 31,360 pixels => 98MB. Reference would do many passes of x (24ch), mid (60ch), mid2 (60ch), out (240ch), shortcut (240ch). That would be (24 + 3*60 + 4*240 + 2*240) * 31k * 4 ≈ ... a lot more. So our fused kernel still saves bandwidth. What about register spilling in Kernel B? Each thread loops over `mid_c` (<=240) and `out_c` (<=960). It needs to store `mid2` (240 floats) temporarily. But we can avoid storing `mid2` explicitly by computing conv3 on the fly? No, conv3 reads shuffled `mid2`. But we can restructure: - For each `o`, we need `dot(w3[o], shuffled_mid2)`. Since `shuffled_mid2` depends on all `mid2` channels. We must compute all `mid2` first. - Could compute `mid2` channel by channel and accumulate into `out`? Since conv3 is 1x1 group, `out[o]` only depends on a subset of shuffled channels (its group's input channels). But the shuffle mixes channels. Actually, group `go` receives shuffled channels `[go, go+3, go+6, ...]` if cpg=20, g=3? No, group `go` receives logical input channels `go*cpg ... (go+1)*cpg - 1`. After shuffle, these logical channels correspond to original channels: For `i = 0..cpg-1`, logical channel `go*cpg + i` -> original `( (go*cpg + i) % g ) * cpg + (go*cpg + i) / g`. So each output group touches a scattered set of original channels across all groups. For example, group 0 logical inputs are shuffled channels `0, 20, 40, 1, 21, 41, ...`. These come from original channels `0, 1, 2, ...`. Actually they come from original channels derived from the formula. Let's compute for g=3, cpg=20: Group 0 logical inputs: `0..19`. `orig_c = (idx%3)*20 + idx/3`. idx=0 -> orig 0. idx=1 -> orig 20. idx=2 -> orig 40. idx=3 -> orig 1. idx=4 -> orig 21. So group 0 touches orig channels from all original groups. Therefore we cannot compute `mid2` incrementally per original group and accumulate to `out`. We need all `mid2` computed beforehand. But we don't need to store all `mid2` in registers. We can compute each `mid2[c]`, then immediately write it to a local array (registers if possible). Since `mid_c` max is 240, and each thread handles one pixel, storing 240 floats is 960 bytes. If the compiler spills, it goes to local memory. Local memory per thread is 240*4=960B. With 256 threads, that's 256KB per block. Shared memory is 48KB, so it can't go there. It will go to global memory (L1 cached). That's still okay given the memory bandwidth. Wait, we don't need shared memory per thread. We can reorder the computation so we don't need to hold all `mid2`. Is it possible? Standard approach: `x = x.view(B, g, i, H*W).permute(0,1,3,2)` -> `[B, g, H*W, i]`. `w = w.view(g, o, i)` -> `[g, o, i]`. Then `out = torch.matmul(x, w.transpose(1,2))`? Let's design the CUDA kernels. **Kernel 1: `shufflenet_conv1_relu`** This is a generic 1x1 group conv + bias + relu. We use 1D block = 256 threads. Grid = `(B*H*W + 255) / 256`. Each thread handles one pixel. ```cuda __global__ void shufflenet_conv1_relu( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W, int in_c, int out_c, int groups) { int total = B * H * W; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W; int h = (idx / W) % H; int b = idx / (H * W); int in_cpg = in_c / groups; int out_cpg = out_c / groups; int base_in = ((b * in_c) * H + h) * W + w; int base_out = ((b * out_c) * H + h) * W + w; for (int o = 0; o < out_c; ++o) { int g = o / out_cpg; float sum = bias[o]; int w_base = o * in_cpg; int in_base = base_in + g * in_cpg * H * W; for (int i = 0; i < in_cpg; ++i) { sum += input[in_base + i * H * W] * weight[w_base + i]; } output[base_out + o * H * W] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } } ``` This is straightforward. The inner loop is small (up to 80). The compiler might unroll it? Not necessarily, but it's fine. **Kernel 2: `shufflenet_depthwise`** Generic 3x3 depthwise + bias. ```cuda __global__ void shufflenet_depthwise( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W, int channels) { int total = B * H * W; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W; int h = (idx / W) % H; int b = idx / (H * W); int base = ((b * channels) * H + h) * W + w; for (int c = 0; c < channels; ++c) { float sum = bias[c]; int w_base = c * 9; #pragma unroll for (int kh = -1; kh <= 1; ++kh) { int h2 = h + kh; if (h2 < 0 || h2 >= H) continue; for (int kw = -1; kw <= 1; ++kw) { int w2 = w + kw; if (w2 < 0 || w2 >= W) continue; int in_idx = ((b * channels + c) * H + h2) * W + w2; int w_idx = w_base + (kh + 1) * 3 + (kw + 1); sum += input[in_idx] * weight[w_idx]; } } output[base + c * H * W] = sum; } } ``` Note: `input` is `mid`, `output` is `mid2`. No activation. **Kernel 3: `shufflenet_conv3_add`** Fuses: channel shuffle (logical) + 1x1 group conv + bias + relu + optional shortcut (1x1 conv + bias) + add. ```cuda __global__ void shufflenet_conv3_add( const float* __restrict__ mid2, const float* __restrict__ x_orig, const float* __restrict__ w3, const float* __restrict__ b3, const float* __restrict__ ws, const float* __restrict__ bs, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int W, int mid_c, int out_c, int groups, int in_c, bool has_shortcut) { int total = B * H * W; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W; int h = (idx / W) % H; int b = idx / (H * W); int mid_cpg = mid_c / groups; int out_cpg = out_c / groups; int base_mid = ((b * mid_c) * H + h) * W + w; int base_out = ((b * out_c) * H + h) * W + w; int base_x = ((b * in_c) * H + h) * W + w; // Precompute shuffled indices? For small mid_c (<=240), compute on the fly. // Local array for shuffled mid2? We can index on the fly. // But we can create a small lookup table in registers if compiler allows. // Since mid_c is small, let's use a local array in shared memory? No, per thread. // We'll just compute the index in the inner loop. for (int o = 0; o < out_c; ++o) { int g = o / out_cpg; float sum = b3[o]; int w_base = o * mid_cpg; for (int i = 0; i < mid_cpg; ++i) { int logical_c = g * mid_cpg + i; int orig_c = (logical_c % groups) * mid_cpg + (logical_c / groups); int mid_idx = base_mid + orig_c * H * W; sum += mid2[mid_idx] * w3[w_base + i]; } // Shortcut if (has_shortcut) { int in_cpg_short = in_c / groups; // Wait, shortcut conv is NOT group conv in the reference! // Reference shortcut: nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, groups=1). // So it's a dense 1x1 conv. float s = bs[o]; for (int i = 0; i < in_c; ++i) { s += x_orig[base_x + i * H * W] * ws[o * in_c + i]; } sum += s; } else { sum += x_orig[base_x + o * H * W]; // identity shortcut, x must have out_c == in_c } output[base_out + o * H * W] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } } ``` Wait, shortcut identity: the reference is `self.shortcut = nn.Sequential()` (identity) when `in_channels == out_channels`. In that case, `x_orig` has `in_c == out_c`. So we add `x_orig[b, o, h, w]`. If `has_shortcut` is true, it's a 1x1 conv. No groups. So it's dense. This kernel is correct but does a lot of work per thread. `out_c` up to 960. For each `o`, inner loop `mid_cpg` up to 80. That's `960*80 = 76,800` FMAs. Plus shortcut dense conv `960*480 = 460,800` FMAs for the first unit of stage4. Total half a million FMAs per pixel. That's heavy. At 19 TFLOPS, half a million FMAs per pixel across 31k pixels is 15.6 billion FMAs = 31 GFLOPs? Wait, 500K * 31,360 = 15.7e9 FMAs = 31.4e9 FLOPs = 31 GFLOPs. At 19 TFLOPS = 1.6ms. That's okay. But register pressure is high. The compiler will likely spill `mid2`? No, here `mid2` is in global memory; we read from it on the fly using computed indices. We don't need a local array! That's the key: we index `mid2` directly via `orig_c`. So no local array. Good. Wait, but we need to read `mid2[base_mid + orig_c * H * W]` inside the `i` loop. The inner loop accesses `mid2` with strides of `mid_cpg * H*W` or so? Let's see: `orig_c = (logical_c % groups) * mid_cpg + (logical_c / groups)`, where `logical_c = g * mid_cpg + i`. As `i` increments, `logical_c` increments by 1. `orig_c` increments by either `1` or something else? For `groups=3`, `mid_cpg=20`: `orig_c(i) = (i % 3)*20 + i/3`. Sequence: i=0 -> 0, i=1 -> 20, i=2 -> 40, i=3 -> 1, i=4 -> 21, i=5 -> 41, i=6 -> 2, ... So `orig_c` jumps by 20, then 20, then -39, then 20, etc. The memory access pattern for `mid2` within the inner loop is strided by `20*H*W` or more. That's terrible for cache within a thread. But the loop is small (<=80). And the total `mid_c` channels is <=240. Total size of `mid2` for one pixel's channels is tiny; for all channels of one pixel it's `240*4=960B`. But the data is not contiguous. However, reading `mid2` for a fixed pixel across all channels means accessing memory addresses `base + c*H*W`. The distance between consecutive channels accessed is `H*W*stride`. If we access channels `0, 20, 40, 1, 21, 41`, the addresses are `base, base+20*H*W, base+40*H*W, base+1*H*W, ...`. These are far apart. They won't hit L1 cache well. They might hit L2 if `H*W` is not too huge. `20*H*W*sizeof(float) = 20*3136*4 = 250KB`. That's larger than L1. So each access is likely an L2 cache miss or hit depending on whether other threads loaded it. But other threads are processing different pixels, so they access different base addresses. No reuse. This kernel might be memory-bound and slow due to strided access. To fix this, we can **pre-shuffle** the `mid2` channels into a local array! If we copy `mid2` into a local array `shuffled[mid_c]` in a separate small loop before the conv3 loop, then the conv3 loop accesses memory contiguously (or at least sequentially from local memory). The copy loop: ```cpp float local_mid[240]; // or dynamic based on mid_c for (int c = 0; c < mid_c; ++c) { int orig_c = (c % groups) * mid_cpg + (c / groups); local_mid[c] = mid2[base_mid + orig_c * H * W]; } ``` Then conv3 uses `local_mid[g*mid_cpg + i]`. This copy does 240 strided reads. Then 960*80 = 76800 contiguous reads from `local_mid`. Since local memory (even if spilled) is cached in L1 per thread? Actually local memory is global memory backed, but contiguous accesses within a thread are coalesced by the memory controller if addresses fall in the same cache line? No, local memory per thread is interleaved or private. On Ampere, local memory accesses go through L1 cache. But they are not coalesced across threads. However, a thread reading its own local memory linearly will have good locality. But can we fit `local_mid[240]` in registers? As discussed, 240 registers is tight. We can try to use `__shared__` memory for this. But each thread needs its own 240 floats. Shared memory is shared by the block. If block size = 32 (warp), 32*240*4 = 30.7KB. Fits in shared memory! But 32 threads means few blocks. Let's revisit tile-based shared memory. **Tile-based Kernel 3:** Block size = `TILE_W` = 32 warps? Actually for shared memory we can have block = (WARPS, something). Let's say block handles `TileX = 4`, `TileY = 8` = 32 pixels. Block size = 256 threads? No, 32 pixels * 8 threads per pixel = 256 threads. Each thread handles one pixel. For each pixel, we need local storage for `mid2` shuffled. Instead of per-thread local, we can compute conv3 without local storage by reordering the weight matrix! Idea: reorder `w3` so that it corresponds to the **original** (unshuffled) channel order. If we define `w3_orig[o, orig_c]` such that `w3_orig[o, orig_c] = w3[o, shuffled_to_logical(orig_c)]`? Let's think. The conv3 operation with shuffle is: `out[o] = sum_{i=0}^{mid_cpg-1} w3[o, i] * mid2[ shuffle_lookup(g*mid_cpg + i) ]` where `shuffle_lookup(c) = (c % groups) * mid_cpg + (c / groups)`. But `mid2` is the depthwise output in original channel order. If we define a new weight matrix `w3_perm` where for each output `o` and each original channel `orig_c`, we set: `w3_perm[o, orig_c] = w3[o, logical_to_shuffled_index(orig_c)]`? Wait, the dot product is over `i` (logical input index to conv3). The mapping from `orig_c` to `i` is deterministic. For a given output group `g`, and logical input index `i`, the original channel is `orig_c(i) = ( (g*mid_cpg + i) % groups ) * mid_cpg + (g*mid_cpg + i) / groups`. But each `orig_c` appears exactly once in the dot product for group `g` (as `i` ranges). Is that true? For fixed `g`, the set `{ orig_c(i) | i=0..mid_cpg-1 }` is a subset of `0..mid_c-1`. Since mapping from `logical_c` to `orig_c` is a bijection on `0..mid_c-1`, for a fixed group `g`, the `orig_c` values are a permutation of size `mid_cpg`. So we can reorder the `w3` weights per output channel to match the original channel order. Specifically, for each output channel `o` (in group `g`), we want `w3_perm[o, orig_c]` to be `w3[o, i]` where `i` is such that `orig_c(i) = orig_c` and `i` in `[0, mid_cpg-1]`. Wait, `orig_c` ranges over `0..mid_c-1`. But `w3[o]` only has `mid_cpg` elements (since it's group conv). So `w3_perm[o, orig_c]` is only non-zero for `orig_c` in the set corresponding to group `g`. We can store `w3_perm` as a dense `[out_c, mid_c]` matrix where each row has `mid_cpg` non-zeros. But that's wasteful. Alternatively, we can pre-shuffle the `mid2` globally once before launching the conv3 kernel. That is, write a kernel that does: - Read `mid2` (original order). - Write `mid2_shuffled` (logical order) using the shuffle mapping. Then conv3 reads `mid2_shuffled` contiguously. This is exactly what the reference does with `.contiguous()`. But the reference's `contiguous()` after transpose does a generic strided copy. Our custom shuffle kernel can be optimized for this specific pattern. It might not be much faster than PyTorch's `contiguous()`, but we save the overhead of creating the view objects and the generic kernel might be as fast. So this brings us back to: either pre-shuffle `mid2` with a custom kernel (or PyTorch contiguous), or reorder weights, or use local memory. Let's estimate the cost of the strided access in the conv3 loop. `mid_cpg` max is 80. For each `o`, we do 80 loads with strides of ~20*H*W. But `H*W=3136`. Stride = ~62720 floats = 250KB. This is large. Memory latency for random access in L2 is maybe 100-200 cycles. 80 loads * 200 cycles = 16,000 cycles per output channel. For 960 output channels, that's huge (15M cycles). At 1.4GHz, that's 10ms per block. Way too slow. So the strided access is unacceptable. We need `mid2` in contiguous logical order for the conv3 dot product. Therefore, we must either: A. Pre-shuffle `mid2` into a local array per thread (registers/local mem). B. Pre-shuffle `mid2` globally with a kernel. C. Reorder `w3` and do something else. Option A: local array `mid2_shuffled[240]`. Copy loop: 240 strided loads (costly, but only 240 per thread). Then 960*80 = 76800 contiguous loads from local array. Register pressure: 240 registers + others. On Ampere, max 255 registers. It might just barely fit. If the compiler spills even one register, it goes to local memory. But local memory is fine for contiguous access. The copy loop is once per thread. Option B: global pre-shuffle kernel. Launch a kernel that writes `mid2_s` contiguously. 1 kernel launch. Then conv3 reads `mid2_s` contiguously. This is what reference does, but our custom shuffle kernel could be faster than generic PyTorch by avoiding overhead? Maybe marginally. But then we have 4 kernels per unit: conv1, depthwise, shuffle, conv3_add. Plus 4 global memory passes. That's okay but not as good. Wait, can we fuse depthwise + shuffle into one kernel that writes shuffled `mid2_s`? Yes! Kernel: per pixel, compute depthwise output for all `mid_c` channels, and store them in `mid2_s` in shuffled order. ```cuda for (int c = 0; c < mid_c; ++c) { float val = ... depthwise ...; int logical_c = (c % groups) * mid_cpg + (c / groups); // Wait, what is the mapping from original c to logical c? } ``` Let's derive the inverse mapping. Original channel `c_orig`. Its logical position after shuffle: In the reference, after shuffle, the order is: orig group0 ch0, orig group1 ch0, orig group2 ch0, orig group0 ch1, orig group1 ch1, orig group2 ch1, ... So `logical_c = (c_orig % cpg) * groups + (c_orig / cpg)`. Check: c_orig=0 -> logical 0. c_orig=1 (group0 ch1) -> logical (1%20)*3 + 1/20 = 1*3 + 0 = 3. Hmm, no. Let's list: groups=3, cpg=20. Original: [g0c0, g0c1, ..., g0c19, g1c0, ..., g1c19, g2c0, ..., g2c19] Shuffled: [g0c0, g1c0, g2c0, g0c1, g1c1, g2c1, ..., g0c19, g1c19, g2c19] So original channel `c_orig` belongs to group `g = c_orig / cpg` and index `i = c_orig % cpg`. Its position in shuffled tensor is `logical_c = i * groups + g`. So `logical_c = (c_orig % cpg) * groups + (c_orig / cpg)`. Check: c_orig=0 -> 0*3+0=0. c_orig=20 (g1c0) -> (20%20)*3 + 1 = 1. c_orig=1 (g0c1) -> 1*3+0=3. Yes. So in the depthwise kernel, after computing `val` for `c_orig`, we can write it to `mid2_s` at `logical_c`. ```cuda int logical_c = (c_orig % mid_cpg) * groups + (c_orig / mid_cpg); output[base_out + logical_c * H * W] = val; ``` Then `mid2_s` is contiguous in logical order. Then the conv3 kernel reads it contiguously. Great! So `unit_depthwise` kernel writes shuffled output directly. This eliminates the need for a separate shuffle kernel or local array. Then `unit_conv3_add` kernel reads `mid2_s` contiguously. ```cuda int base_mid = ((b * mid_c) * H + h) * W + w; for (int o = 0; o < out_c; ++o) { int g = o / out_cpg; float sum = b3[o]; int w_base = o * mid_cpg; int mid_base = base_mid + g * mid_cpg * H * W; for (int i = 0; i < mid_cpg; ++i) { sum += mid2[mid_base + i * H * W] * w3[w_base + i]; } ... } ``` Now `mid2` is read contiguously per group! Excellent. So our kernels are: - `shufflenet_conv1`: 1x1 group conv + bias + relu -> writes `mid`. - `shufflenet_depthwise_shuffle`: 3x3 depthwise + bias, writes output in shuffled channel order -> writes `mid_s`. - `shufflenet_conv3_add`: 1x1 group conv + bias + relu + shortcut + add -> writes `out`. This is 3 kernels per unit, each simple and memory-coalesced. Can we merge `conv1` and `depthwise_shuffle` into one kernel? We would need to write `mid` to global memory because depthwise needs neighbors. Unless we tile spatially. But 3 kernels is fine. What about the very first `conv1+bn1+relu` of the network? It's a regular conv `3->24`, kernel 3x3, stride 2. The reference uses `F.relu(self.bn1(self.conv1(x)))`. We can fuse the weights and just call `F.conv2d(x, w_fused, b_fused)` followed by `F.relu_`. Or we could write a custom kernel for it. Writing a custom kernel for 3x3 conv with stride 2 is more complex. `F.conv2d` is fine. The input is large (224x224) but channels are small. It won't be the bottleneck. Similarly, `maxpool` is standard. `conv5` is 1x1, 960->1024. We can use `F.conv2d`. `fc` is linear. `F.linear` or `torch.matmul`. Actually, for `conv5`, we can fuse bn+relu into weights too. `x = F.relu(F.conv2d(x, w5, b5))`. If we use `cudnn_convolution_relu`, even better. Then `x = F.adaptive_avg_pool2d(x, (1,1))`. This can be replaced with `x.mean(dim=(2,3), keepdim=True)` or `x.mean(dim=(2,3))`. Then `x = x.view(x.size(0), -1)`, then `self.fc(x)`. So the `ModelNew.forward` would be: - F.conv2d (conv1 fused) - F.relu_ - F.max_pool2d - For each unit in stages: - conv1_kernel - depthwise_kernel - conv3_add_kernel - F.conv2d (conv5 fused) - F.relu_ - mean over HW - view - F.linear Now, the main challenge is writing the CUDA kernels correctly and efficiently with `load_inline`. Let's think about the kernel template code. We need to declare 3 CUDA functions and bind them. Because the channel dimensions vary per unit, we pass them as arguments. `shufflenet_conv1_relu`: Parameters: - `input` (B, in_c, H, W) - `weight` (out_c, in_c/g, 1, 1) - `bias` (out_c) - `output` (B, out_c, H, W) - `B, H, W, in_c, out_c, groups` `shufflenet_depthwise_shuffle`: Parameters: - `input` (B, mid_c, H, W) - `weight` (mid_c, 1, 3, 3) - `bias` (mid_c) - `output` (B, mid_c, H, W) (shuffled) - `B, H, W, mid_c, groups` Note: `weight` is stored as `[mid_c, 1, 3, 3]` which is `[mid_c, 3, 3]`. We can flatten to `[mid_c, 9]`. `shufflenet_conv3_add`: Parameters: - `input` (B, mid_c, H, W) (shuffled) - `x_orig` (B, in_c, H, W) - `w3` (out_c, mid_c/g, 1, 1) - `b3` (out_c) - `ws` (out_c, in_c, 1, 1) or None - `bs` (out_c) or None - `output` (B, out_c, H, W) - `B, H, W, mid_c, out_c, groups, in_c, has_shortcut` Wait, for identity shortcut, we just add `x_orig`. No, `x_orig` is the input to the unit. In the kernel, if not `has_shortcut`, we read `x_orig[base_x + o * H * W]`. If `has_shortcut`, we compute a dense 1x1 conv on `x_orig`. But computing a dense 1x1 conv in the same per-pixel thread is a lot of FMAs (out_c * in_c). For 24->240 unit: `240*24 = 5,760`. For 480->960: `960*480 = 460,800`. This might be okay. However, writing `ws` as a dense `[out_c, in_c]` weight matrix. PyTorch conv2d weight for 1x1 is `[out_c, in_c, 1, 1]`. We can flatten to `[out_c, in_c]`. One issue: `has_shortcut` parameter is a bool. In CUDA, we can pass as `int`. **Weight pre-processing in Python:** For each `ShuffleNetUnit`, we extract `conv1.weight`, `bn1` stats, compute fused `w1` and `b1`. Similarly for `bn2` (no relu), `conv3+bn3+relu`, and `shortcut` (if present). Then we pass these fused tensors to the kernels. Wait, `conv3` output goes through `bn3` and `relu`. We can fuse `bn3` into `conv3` weights and bias, and then apply relu in the kernel. So the kernel does `relu(conv3(x) + shortcut)`. Similarly, `conv1` output goes through `bn1` and `relu`. Fuse into kernel. `conv2` output goes through `bn2` (no relu). Fuse bias. `shortcut` is `conv_s` -> `bn_s`. Fuse into weights and bias. So the kernel signatures are correct. **Implementation details for weight fusion:** For a conv layer with weight `W` [out_c, in_c/g, k, k] and BN with `gamma`, `beta`, `running_mean`, `running_var`, `eps`: `w_fused = W * (gamma / sqrt(var+eps)).view(out_c, 1, 1, 1)` `b_fused = beta - gamma * mean / sqrt(var+eps)` If conv originally had bias, we would add it, but all convs are `bias=False`. So `b_fused` is just that. If we also fuse ReLU, we don't change weights; the kernel applies `relu` after adding bias. So for `conv1+bn1+relu`: fuse weight and bias. For `conv2+bn2`: fuse weight and bias. For `conv3+bn3+relu`: fuse weight and bias. For `shortcut_conv+bn`: fuse weight and bias. Note: `conv2` is depthwise. Weight shape `[mid_c, 1, 3, 3]`. BN stats shape `[mid_c]`. Fused weight: multiply each filter by its channel's gamma/sqrt(var+eps). `w2_fused[c, :, :] = w2[c, :, :] * (gamma[c] / sqrt(...))` `b2_fused[c] = beta[c] - gamma[c] * mean[c] / sqrt(...)` **Potential issue:** `load_inline` with multiple functions. We can define the three kernels in the `cuda_sources` string. Then define wrapper functions in `cpp_sources` that call the kernels. We should specify `functions=['shufflenet_conv1_relu', 'shufflenet_depthwise_shuffle', 'shufflenet_conv3_add']`? Actually, we define host C++ functions with those names that take `at::Tensor` and dispatch CUDA. `load_inline` will generate pybind11 for these functions.
